This discovery was made possible by the concerted efforts of mineral physics scientists around the world, as they sought to increase the range and quality of LHDAC experiments and as "ab initio" calculations attained predictive power.



Another important advancement is the improved mapping of seismic discontinuities in the lower mantle, which has provided valuable information about the distribution and properties of different phase transitions. This has allowed for a better understanding of the dynamics and evolution of the lower mantle.



### Subsection: 16.2c Future Directions in Phase Transitions in Lower Mantle Research



While significant progress has been made in understanding phase transitions in the lower mantle, there are still many important questions that remain unanswered. One of the key areas for future research is the determination of the Clapeyron slope for different phase transitions in the lower mantle. This parameter is crucial for understanding the behavior of these transitions and its accurate determination is essential for accurate modeling of the lower mantle.



Another important direction for future research is the investigation of the effects of minor elements, such as water, on phase transitions in the lower mantle. These elements can significantly alter the melting behavior of major mineral phases and thus impact the overall solidus temperature. Understanding these effects is crucial for accurately modeling the dynamics and evolution of the lower mantle.



In conclusion, the study of phase transitions in the lower mantle is a rapidly advancing field that has provided valuable insights into the dynamics and evolution of the Earth's interior. With continued research and advancements in experimental and theoretical techniques, we can expect to gain a deeper understanding of these important processes in the future.





# Structure and Dynamics of the CMB Region



## Chapter 16: Lower-Mantle Solidus Revisited



### Section: 16.3 Implications of Lower-Mantle Solidus Revisited



#### Subsection: 16.3a Current Theories on the Implications of Lower-Mantle Solidus



The lower mantle solidus is a critical parameter in understanding the dynamics and evolution of the core-mantle boundary (CMB) region. It is the temperature at which the lower mantle begins to melt, and it is influenced by various factors such as pressure, composition, and the presence of minor elements. In this section, we will discuss the current theories on the implications of the lower mantle solidus and how it affects the structure and dynamics of the CMB region.



One of the main implications of the lower mantle solidus is its role in the formation and evolution of the Earth's core. As the lower mantle begins to melt, the liquid iron migrates downward to form the liquid outer core, while the solidified silicates form the solid lower mantle. This process is thought to have occurred early in the Earth's history, and it is believed to have played a crucial role in the differentiation of the Earth into its distinct layers.



Another implication of the lower mantle solidus is its influence on the physical and chemical properties of the lower mantle. As the temperature increases and the lower mantle begins to melt, the density, viscosity, and thermal conductivity of the material change. This can have significant effects on the dynamics of the CMB region, such as the convection of the liquid outer core and the movement of the solid lower mantle.



Current theories suggest that the melting behavior of major mineral phases, such as bridgmanite and ferropericlase, plays a crucial role in controlling the lower mantle solidus. These minerals have different melting points, and their relative proportions in the lower mantle can affect the overall solidus temperature. Additionally, the presence of minor elements, such as water, can also influence the melting behavior of these minerals and thus impact the lower mantle solidus.



Understanding the implications of the lower mantle solidus is crucial for gaining a better understanding of the Earth's interior and its evolution over time. Recent advancements in experimental techniques and computational models have allowed for more accurate measurements and predictions of the lower mantle solidus. However, there is still much to be learned about this critical parameter and its role in the structure and dynamics of the CMB region. Further research and studies in this area will continue to deepen our understanding of the Earth's interior and its complex processes.





# Structure and Dynamics of the CMB Region



## Chapter 16: Lower-Mantle Solidus Revisited



### Section: 16.3 Implications of Lower-Mantle Solidus Revisited



#### Subsection: 16.3b Recent Findings on the Implications of Lower-Mantle Solidus



In recent years, there have been significant advancements in our understanding of the implications of the lower mantle solidus. These findings have shed light on the complex dynamics and evolution of the CMB region, providing new insights into the formation and structure of the Earth.



One of the most significant recent findings is the role of minor elements, particularly water, in controlling the lower mantle solidus. Studies have shown that the presence of even small amounts of water can significantly lower the solidus temperature of major mineral phases, such as bridgmanite and ferropericlase. This has important implications for the dynamics of the CMB region, as the addition of water can lead to increased melting and changes in the physical and chemical properties of the lower mantle.



Another recent development is the discovery of large low-shear-velocity provinces (LLSVPs) in the lowermost mantle. These slow features, located beneath Africa and the Pacific, have been found to have a strong influence on the structure and dynamics of the CMB region. The boundaries of these features align with the smallest moments of inertia of the Earth, suggesting a connection between the LLSVPs and the Earth's rotation.



Furthermore, seismological constraints have provided valuable insights into the structure and composition of the lower mantle. By analyzing shear velocity models, researchers have been able to identify the boundaries of the LLSVPs and gain a better understanding of their properties. This has led to the development of new theories on the formation and evolution of these features, providing a deeper understanding of the dynamics of the CMB region.



Overall, recent findings on the implications of the lower mantle solidus have greatly enhanced our understanding of the CMB region. These advancements have highlighted the complex interplay between various factors, such as pressure, composition, and the presence of minor elements, in controlling the dynamics and evolution of the Earth's core-mantle boundary. As research in this field continues, we can expect to gain even more insights into the structure and dynamics of the CMB region, furthering our understanding of the Earth's interior.





# Structure and Dynamics of the CMB Region



## Chapter 16: Lower-Mantle Solidus Revisited



### Section: 16.3 Implications of Lower-Mantle Solidus Revisited



#### Subsection: 16.3c Future Directions in the Implications of Lower-Mantle Solidus Research



As our understanding of the lower mantle solidus continues to evolve, it is important to consider the future directions of research in this field. In this subsection, we will discuss some potential avenues for future studies and their potential implications for our understanding of the CMB region.



One area of interest is the role of minor elements in controlling the lower mantle solidus. While studies have shown that water can significantly lower the solidus temperature, there are still many unanswered questions about the exact mechanisms and processes involved. Further research in this area could provide valuable insights into the dynamics of the CMB region and the role of water in the Earth's interior.



Another promising direction for future research is the study of the effects of pressure on the lower mantle solidus. While much of the current research has focused on the effects of temperature, pressure also plays a crucial role in the formation and evolution of the CMB region. By studying the effects of pressure on the solidus, we can gain a better understanding of the complex interactions between temperature, pressure, and composition in the lower mantle.



In addition, there is still much to be learned about the large low-shear-velocity provinces (LLSVPs) in the lowermost mantle. While their boundaries have been identified, their exact composition and origin are still a topic of debate. Further research on the LLSVPs could provide valuable insights into the dynamics of the CMB region and the Earth's interior.



Furthermore, the use of seismological constraints has proven to be a powerful tool in studying the lower mantle solidus. However, there is still much to be learned about the limitations and uncertainties of these methods. Future research could focus on improving the accuracy and resolution of seismological data, providing a more detailed understanding of the structure and composition of the lower mantle.



Finally, it is important to consider the implications of the lower mantle solidus for the future of Earth. As tectonic events continue to shape the surface of our planet, it is crucial to understand the underlying processes and dynamics that drive these changes. By studying the lower mantle solidus, we can gain a better understanding of the long-term evolution of the Earth and the potential impact of these processes on our planet's future.



In conclusion, the implications of the lower mantle solidus are a rich and complex field of study with many potential avenues for future research. By continuing to explore these topics, we can gain a deeper understanding of the structure and dynamics of the CMB region and the Earth's interior. 





# Structure and Dynamics of the CMB Region



## Chapter 16: Lower-Mantle Solidus Revisited



### Section: 16.4 Mineralogy and Crystallography of Lower Mantle Revisited



In the previous section, we discussed the implications of the lower mantle solidus and its potential role in the dynamics of the CMB region. In this section, we will delve deeper into the mineralogy and crystallography of the lower mantle and how it relates to our understanding of the solidus.



The lower mantle is mainly composed of three major minerals: bridgmanite, ferropericlase, and calcium-silicate perovskite (CaSiO<sub>3</sub>-perovskite). These minerals have been extensively studied through laboratory experiments and seismological observations. However, the exact proportion of each mineral in the lower mantle is still a subject of debate.



Laboratory experiments have simulated the conditions of the lower mantle and measured the density using "in situ" X-ray diffraction. These experiments have shown that the density profile along the geotherm is in agreement with the Preliminary Reference Earth Model (PREM). Furthermore, first principle calculations of the density and velocity profile across the lower mantle geotherm have observed a match to the PREM model at an 8:2 proportion of bridgmanite and ferropericlase. This proportion is consistent with the pyrolitic bulk composition at the lower mantle.



However, recent studies have also suggested that the lower mantle may be composed of a greater proportion of bridgmanite, up to 93%. This is consistent with a chondritic lower mantle composition. These findings have sparked further research into the mineralogy and crystallography of the lower mantle, particularly in understanding the role of minor elements and their effects on the solidus.



One promising area of research is the use of advanced techniques in studying lower mantle mineralogy. These techniques include Brillouin spectroscopy, which has been used to study the shear-wave velocities of lower mantle compositions. Studies have shown that a lower mantle composed of greater than 93% bridgmanite phase has corresponding shear-wave velocities to measured seismic velocities. This suggests that the composition of the lower mantle may be more chondritic than previously thought.



In addition, the study of post-perovskite phase is also crucial in understanding the mineralogy and crystallography of the lower mantle. This phase is stable above 120 GPa at 2500 K and is thought to be the origin of seismic discontinuities in the D" region. Further research on post-perovskite can provide valuable insights into the dynamics of the CMB region and the Earth's interior.



As our understanding of the lower mantle solidus continues to evolve, it is important to consider the future directions of research in this field. One potential avenue is the study of the effects of pressure on the solidus, as pressure plays a crucial role in the formation and evolution of the CMB region. Additionally, further research on the large low-shear-velocity provinces (LLSVPs) in the lowermost mantle can provide valuable insights into the composition and origin of these enigmatic regions.



In conclusion, the mineralogy and crystallography of the lower mantle are crucial in understanding the dynamics of the CMB region and the Earth's interior. Through advanced techniques and further research, we can continue to refine our understanding of the lower mantle solidus and its implications for the structure and dynamics of the CMB region.





# Structure and Dynamics of the CMB Region



## Chapter 16: Lower-Mantle Solidus Revisited



### Section: 16.4 Mineralogy and Crystallography of Lower Mantle Revisited



In the previous section, we discussed the implications of the lower mantle solidus and its potential role in the dynamics of the CMB region. In this section, we will delve deeper into the mineralogy and crystallography of the lower mantle and how it relates to our understanding of the solidus.



The lower mantle is mainly composed of three major minerals: bridgmanite, ferropericlase, and calcium-silicate perovskite (CaSiO<sub>3</sub>-perovskite). These minerals have been extensively studied through laboratory experiments and seismological observations. However, the exact proportion of each mineral in the lower mantle is still a subject of debate.



Laboratory experiments have simulated the conditions of the lower mantle and measured the density using "in situ" X-ray diffraction. These experiments have shown that the density profile along the geotherm is in agreement with the Preliminary Reference Earth Model (PREM). Furthermore, first principle calculations of the density and velocity profile across the lower mantle geotherm have observed a match to the PREM model at an 8:2 proportion of bridgmanite and ferropericlase. This proportion is consistent with the pyrolitic bulk composition at the lower mantle.

These findings have sparked further research into the mineralogy and crystallography of the lower mantle, particularly in understanding the role of minor elements and their effects on the solidus.



One promising area of research is the use of advanced techniques in studying lower mantle mineralogy. These techniques include Brillouin spectroscopy, which has been used to study the shear-wave velocities of lower mantle minerals. This has provided valuable insights into the elastic properties of bridgmanite and ferropericlase, and how they may contribute to the dynamics of the CMB region.



Another important aspect of lower mantle mineralogy is the presence of post-perovskite. This phase is stable at depths of about 2600 km and is thought to be responsible for the D" seismic discontinuity. Recent studies have shown that the post-perovskite phase transition can also be affected by the presence of minor elements, such as iron and aluminum. This has led to a better understanding of the dynamics of the CMB region and the potential role of post-perovskite in driving mantle convection.



In addition to mineralogy, the crystallography of lower mantle minerals is also a topic of interest. Recent studies have shown that the crystal structure of bridgmanite can change under high pressures and temperatures, leading to different elastic properties. This has important implications for our understanding of the solidus and the dynamics of the CMB region.



Overall, the mineralogy and crystallography of the lower mantle are crucial in understanding the structure and dynamics of the CMB region. Further research in this area will continue to provide valuable insights into the composition and behavior of the lower mantle, and how it contributes to the overall dynamics of the Earth's interior.





# Structure and Dynamics of the CMB Region



## Chapter 16: Lower-Mantle Solidus Revisited



### Section: 16.4 Mineralogy and Crystallography of Lower Mantle Revisited



In the previous section, we discussed the implications of the lower mantle solidus and its potential role in the dynamics of the CMB region. In this section, we will delve deeper into the mineralogy and crystallography of the lower mantle and how it relates to our understanding of the solidus.



The lower mantle is mainly composed of three major minerals: bridgmanite, ferropericlase, and calcium-silicate perovskite (CaSiO<sub>3</sub>-perovskite). These minerals have been extensively studied through laboratory experiments and seismological observations. However, the exact proportion of each mineral in the lower mantle is still a subject of debate.



Laboratory experiments have simulated the conditions of the lower mantle and measured the density using "in situ" X-ray diffraction. These experiments have shown that the density profile along the geotherm is in agreement with the Preliminary Reference Earth Model (PREM). Furthermore, first principle calculations of the density and velocity profile across the lower mantle geotherm have observed a match to the PREM model at an 8:2 proportion of bridgmanite and ferropericlase. This proportion is consistent with the pyrolitic bulk composition at the lower mantle.

These findings have sparked further research into the mineralogy and crystallography of the lower mantle, particularly in understanding the role of minor elements and their effects on the solidus.



One promising area of research is the use of advanced techniques in studying lower mantle mineralogy. These techniques include Brillouin spectroscopy, which has been used to study the shear-wave velocities of lower mantle minerals. This method has provided valuable insights into the elastic properties of bridgmanite and ferropericlase, and has also revealed the presence of minor elements such as iron and aluminum in these minerals.



Another important aspect of lower mantle mineralogy is the crystallographic orientation of these minerals. Recent studies have shown that the crystallographic orientation of bridgmanite and ferropericlase can affect the strength and deformation behavior of these minerals. This has significant implications for the dynamics of the lower mantle and the potential role of these minerals in mantle convection.



In addition to studying the mineralogy and crystallography of the lower mantle, there is also ongoing research into the effects of pressure and temperature on these minerals. As we continue to improve our understanding of the lower mantle solidus, it is important to consider the effects of these variables on the mineralogy and crystallography of the lower mantle. This will provide valuable insights into the dynamics of the CMB region and the processes that occur within it.



Looking towards the future, there are many exciting directions for research in lower mantle mineralogy. One area of interest is the study of the post-perovskite phase and its potential role in the dynamics of the lower mantle. As mentioned in the related context, the post-perovskite phase has been linked to seismic discontinuities in the D" layer, and further research into this phase could provide valuable insights into the structure and dynamics of the CMB region.



Another promising direction is the use of advanced experimental techniques, such as synchrotron X-ray diffraction and neutron scattering, to study the mineralogy and crystallography of the lower mantle. These techniques allow for more precise measurements and can provide valuable information about the behavior of minerals under extreme conditions.



In conclusion, the mineralogy and crystallography of the lower mantle are crucial components in our understanding of the dynamics of the CMB region. Ongoing research in this field will continue to provide valuable insights into the structure and behavior of the lower mantle, and will ultimately contribute to our understanding of the Earth's interior.
